    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="4ac1d2365a56440e93f3a62975bb6c30">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'caec56da-92f0-4e25-84db-75953f353fd0'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Chen/Muqing">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Zhu/Yanwu" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a6" data-db-target-for="a6" aria-controls="a6" aria-haspopup="true" id="a6_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Yanwu Zhu<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a6" aria-labelledby="a6_Ctrl" role="region" id="a6">
                     <p class="author-type mb-1">Corresponding Author</p>
                     <p class="author-name">Yanwu Zhu</p>

                     </ul>
                     <p>Hefei National Laboratory for Physical Sciences at Microscale, Key Laboratory of Precision and Intelligent Chemistry, Department of Materials Science and, Engineering, University of Science and Technology of China, Hefei, 230026 P. R. China</p>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d145876619" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>2D layered MXene-based materials are applied as cation-intercalation electrode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) due to their layered structures but suffer from spontaneous restacking during Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and deintercalation processes, resulting in sluggish reaction kinetics and poor cycling stability. Herein, fullerene C<sub>60</sub> is intercalated covalently into the interlayer of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>x</sub> MXene nanosheets by using a low-temperature hydrothermal reaction between a water-soluble C<sub>60</sub> derivative and hydrophilic MXene nanosheets, resulting in enlarged interlayer spacing of MXene nanosheets from 12.8 to 14.1&nbsp;Å and consequently retarded self-restacking. Moreover, the strong electron extraction ability of C<sub>60</sub> facilitates electron transfer from MXene to C<sub>60</sub>, enabling faster charge transport during Na<sup>+</sup> transportation. The as-prepared C<sub>60</sub>@MXene hybrid is applied as a novel anode of SIBs, exhibiting outstanding electrochemical performance and super-long cycling stability. C<sub>60</sub>@MXene-based SIB delivers a specific capacity of 226.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;A g<sup>−1</sup> after 300 cycles, which surpasses that obtained from the pristine MXene anode, and retains 94.5% capacity at 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 10&nbsp;000 cycles. DFT simulations confirm that C<sub>60</sub>-induced enlarged interlayer spacing benefits Na<sup>+</sup> migrations, which is responsible for improved electrochemical performance and cycling stability.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202400185-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202400185-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Rechargeable batteries play a crucial role in energy storage applications toward growing demand for renewable energy.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0001_R_d145876601e479" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Energy">1</a>-<a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0003_R_d145876601e479" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Energy">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Currently, lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have been widely used in commercial applications. However, the limited Li resources in the Earth's crust along with its high cost make it difficult to meet the demand for large-scale energy storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0004_R_d145876601e484" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, it is an urgent task to develop cost-effective next-generation large-scale sustainable energy storage systems.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0005_R_d145876601e489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Chem. Rev.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Emerging sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been considered as potential candidates due to their low cost, and abundant sodium resources.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0006_R_d145876601e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Energy Chem.">6</a>-<a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0010_R_d145876601e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, Na<sup>+</sup> with a larger ion radius (1.02&nbsp;Å) than Li<sup>+</sup> (0.76&nbsp;Å) and larger atomic weight may induce volume expansion during Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction processes within the interlayers of anode materials with relatively small transport pathway, resulting in sluggish reaction kinetics, unsatisfying rate performance, and poor cycling performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0011_R_d145876601e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">11</a>-<a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0014_R_d145876601e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, developing high-performance anode materials enabling stable and rapid Na<sup>+</sup> transportation during long-term cycles is highly desired.</p>
         
         <p>MXenes as a broad category of 2D transition metal carbides or nitrides have been applied as suitable electrode materials for energy storage owing to their unique physicochemical properties, such as high electrical conductivity, rich surficial groups, and capability to host a broad range of intercalants.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0015_R_d145876601e517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nat. Rev. Chem.">15</a>-<a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0023_R_d145876601e517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Mater. Today Sustain.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> MXenes are represented by a general formula of M<sub>n+1</sub>X<sub>n</sub>T<sub>x</sub> (where M is the former transition metal from IIIB to VIB, X is C and/or N element, n&nbsp;=&nbsp;1–3, and T is the terminal surface functional groups such as ─OH, ═O or ─F) are mainly fabricated through selective etching of the “A” layers (A is mainly a group IIIA or IVA element) from layered ternary carbides or carbonitrides named as MAX.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0024_R_d145876601e529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Mater.">24</a>-<a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0028_R_d145876601e529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Nat. Energy">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Particularly, laminated Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>x</sub> MXene is regarded as an appealing SIB anode material on account of its large surface area, tunable interlayer spacing, high electrical conductivity, high volumetric capacity, and low Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion barrier.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0029_R_d145876601e543" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - ACS Nano">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the adjacent MXene layers will spontaneously restack during Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and deintercalation processes due to van der Waals interactions and/or hydrogen bonds, hindering ion accessibility and leading to the loss of redox-active sites and sluggish reaction kinetics, thus limiting its electrochemical performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0030_R_d145876601e550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To address this issue, enlarging interlayer spacing has been demonstrated as an effective strategy, which can be accomplished intuitively by intercalating small-size ions, atoms, or nanomaterials to construct pillared or sandwich-like hybrid materials with Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>x</sub> MXene.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0031_R_d145876601e562" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Mater. Chem. A">31</a>-<a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0033_R_d145876601e562" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For instance, alkali metal ion pillaring was applied for Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>x</sub> MXene, and Na<sup>+</sup> pillaring renders increased interlayer spacing, affording a high reversible capacity of 175&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, which reached ≈170% of the original value.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0031_R_d145876601e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Mater. Chem. A">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene/carbon nanotube (CNT) composite with weak non-covalent interactions between CNT and Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> layers was prepared and applied as the anode of SIB, affording a volumetric capacity of 421&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−3</sup> at 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with a continuous capacity decrease after 200 cycles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0032_R_d145876601e599" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Nano Energy">32</a>, <a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0033_R_d145876601e599" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Despite the effectiveness of such intercalation strategies in improving the SIB anode performance of MXene, the intercalants are mostly incorporated non-covalently and render weak interactions with MXene, raising insufficient long-term stability issues. In this sense, covalently intercalating MXene by functional molecules appears challenging.</p>
         
         <p>To develop new intercalants that may render covalent and strong interactions with MXene without significant interference with its chemical structure, molecular nanomaterials such as fullerene C<sub>60</sub> with a precise van der Waals diameter of ≈0.71&nbsp;nm appear to be ideal choices. C<sub>60</sub> and its derivatives have been already applied in rechargeable batteries due to their excellent electron extraction ability and abundant redox active sites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0034_R_d145876601e611" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Nanoscale">34</a>-<a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0038_R_d145876601e611" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Nano Res.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Feng et&nbsp;al. prepared a Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>x</sub> MXene/C<sub>60</sub> hybrid film with the assistance of water-soluble γ-cyclodextrin (CD) as a dispersing agent and applied it in electromagnetic interference (EMI) shielding. C<sub>60</sub> molecules are non-covalently intercalated within MXene interlayers and need dispersion of CD.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0039_R_d145876601e627" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Mater. Sci. Technol.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Very recently, Awan et&nbsp;al. used the pristine C<sub>60</sub> to non-covalently intercalate the MXene layers by a hydrothermal method, affording improved capacitance of MXene-based supercapacitors due to increased interlayer spacing.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0040_R_d145876601e635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - ACS Omega">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, none of these reports realizes the C<sub>60</sub> covalent intercalation of MXene.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, for the first time, fullerene C<sub>60</sub> molecules are covalently intercalated into the interlayer of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>x</sub> MXene nanosheets by using a low-temperature hydrothermal method, through a condensation reaction between a water-soluble C<sub>60</sub> derivative and hydrophilic MXene nanosheets. A series of spectroscopic and morphological characterizations are carried out to ascertain the successful intercalation of C<sub>60</sub> as pillars via N─Ti bonds. C<sub>60</sub> intercalation results in enlarged interlayer spacing of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>x</sub> MXene nanosheets and consequently retarding self-restacking. Upon applying the as-prepared C<sub>60</sub>@MXene hybrid as the novel anode of SIBs, obviously, improved specific capacity and cycling stability relative to the pristine MXene anode are achieved, attributed to the facilitated Na<sup>+</sup> migrations resulting from C<sub>60</sub>-induced enlarged interlayer spacing as confirmed by DFT simulations.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202400185-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202400185-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202400185-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202400185-sec-0030-title">2.1 Synthesis and Structure Characterizations of C<sub>60</sub>@MXene Hybrid</h3>
            
            <p>The synthetic route of C<sub>60</sub>@MXene is illustrated in <b>Scheme</b> <a href="#adfm202400185-fig-0006">1</a>. Few-layered Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>x</sub> MXene (abbreviated as MXene) was prepared by HCl/LiF etching the Al layers of MAX phase based on Ti<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> following the procedure reported in literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0041_R_d145876601e707" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Energy Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The obtained MXene nanosheets are hydrophilic because of the abundant oxygen-containing terminal functional groups (─OH,═O). The water-soluble fullerene derivative (abbreviated as f-C<sub>60</sub>) with a molecular formula of C<sub>60</sub>O<sub>∼10</sub>(OH)<sub>∼14</sub>(NH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>∼7</sub>, which is determined by XPS, was synthesized by a one-step reaction of C<sub>60</sub> with H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and NH<sub>3</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0042_R_d145876601e743" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Mater. Chem. A">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A low-temperature (95&nbsp;°C) hydrothermal method has been used to construct covalently hybridized C<sub>60</sub>@MXene through a condensation reaction between hydrophilic MXene nanosheets and f-C<sub>60</sub> molecules.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202400185-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cc878939-2ba5-4ff0-b2ce-bc38219f3c6f/adfm202400185-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/cc878939-2ba5-4ff0-b2ce-bc38219f3c6f/adfm202400185-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3fec7b44-15ad-403e-9588-2a9cd0a1dc0f/adfm202400185-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cc878939-2ba5-4ff0-b2ce-bc38219f3c6f/adfm202400185-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Scheme 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202400185-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202400185" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustration of the preparation process of C<sub>60</sub>@MXene hybrid.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Scanning electron microscopic (SEM) and transmission electron microscopic (TEM) characterizations were conducted to get a deep insight into the exfoliation process of MXene (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202400185-fig-0001">1</a>, see also Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2, S3</a>, Supporting Information). Al layers in Ti<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> are etched after HCl/LiF treatment to form an exfoliated layered structure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400185-fig-0001">1a</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a,b</a>, Supporting Information). After the incorporation of C<sub>60</sub>, the nanosheets are further delaminated and become more dispersed and independent (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400185-fig-0001">1b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2c</a>, Supporting Information). Looking at TEM images, C<sub>60</sub>@MXene nanosheets are much rougher, thinner, and more dispersed than the pristine MXene, consistent with the SEM results (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400185-fig-0001">1c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3a,b</a>, Supporting Information). Besides, some spherical nanoparticles correspond to C<sub>60</sub> clusters bonded at the MXene surface.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202400185-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/20f2301f-135a-48d0-a00f-1c930bce5162/adfm202400185-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/20f2301f-135a-48d0-a00f-1c930bce5162/adfm202400185-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ff893279-4d14-489e-8dba-c566dad724e5/adfm202400185-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/20f2301f-135a-48d0-a00f-1c930bce5162/adfm202400185-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202400185-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202400185" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">SEM images of a) the pristine MXene and b) C<sub>60</sub>@MXene nanosheets. c) TEM image of C<sub>60</sub>@MXene. The dotted circles mark C<sub>60</sub> clusters bonded at the MXene surface. HR-TEM images of d) the pristine MXene and e) C<sub>60</sub>@MXene. f) Enlarged view of the area selected in orange square in e). g) STEM image of C<sub>60</sub>@MXene and the corresponding EDS mapping of C, N, O, F, and Ti elements.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The changes in thickness and dispersibility of MXene and C<sub>60</sub>@MXene were characterized by Atomic force microscopy (AFM). After C<sub>60</sub> intercalating, multiple layers of MXene with a thickness of ≈18&nbsp;nm were stripped into a few layers or even monolayers with a small thickness of 3.9&nbsp;nm (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0043_R_d145876601e855" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Nanoscale">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> indicating that C<sub>60</sub>@MXene nanosheets are thinner and more dispersed than the pristine MXene. Dispersions of MXene and C<sub>60</sub>@MXene in water were monitored to visually compare their dispersibilities (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). C<sub>60</sub>@MXene remains well dispersed in water after standing for 24&nbsp;h, whereas the pristine MXene sinks already after 12&nbsp;h and precipitates completely after 24&nbsp;h. This further confirms the improved dispersibility of C<sub>60</sub>@MXene.</p>
            
            <p>According to a comparison of the high-resolution TEM (HR-TEM) images of the pristine MXene and C<sub>60</sub>@MXene hybrid illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400185-fig-0001">1d,e</a>, the interlayer distance of 12.3&nbsp;Å observed for the pristine MXene increases to 13.2&nbsp;Å in C<sub>60</sub>@MXene hybrid, revealing that C<sub>60</sub> molecules are intercalated into the interlayer of MXene nanosheets, leading to enlarged interlayer spacing of MXene nanosheets.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0044_R_d145876601e885" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Nat. Commun.">44</a>, <a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0045_R_d145876601e885" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - J. Mater. Chem. A">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The C<sub>60</sub> clusters can be observed both on the surface and edge of MXene nanosheets. As observed in the HR-TEM image of the C<sub>60</sub>@MXene hybrid shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400185-fig-0001">1e</a>, the lattice fringes of 3.5&nbsp;Å can be ascribed to the short-range ordering of f-C<sub>60</sub> molecules, confirming the distribution of f-C<sub>60</sub> molecules at the surface of MXene nanosheets.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0046_R_d145876601e903" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Appl. Catal. B Environ.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the overlap of lattice stripes in two directions is observed in the magnified HR-TEM image in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400185-fig-0001">1f</a>, in which the lattice fringes of 2.3&nbsp;Å correspond to the arrangement of Ti atoms on each MXene layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0024_R_d145876601e911" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This phenomenon indicates that C<sub>60</sub> molecules are bonded with the Ti atoms at the edge of MXene layers. The insertion of C<sub>60</sub> clusters or molecules can lift off the surface layers (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3c</a>, Supporting Information) and distort the MXene layer's structure (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3d</a>, Supporting Information). On the basis of energy dispersive X-ray (EDX) elemental mapping analysis shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400185-fig-0001">1g</a>, except for C, O, F, and Ti elements available in MXene (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting information), N element is also detected for C<sub>60</sub>@MXene hybrid, confirming the successful incorporation of C<sub>60</sub> moiety.</p>
            
            <p>We then employed X-ray powder diffraction (XRD) to probe the effect of C<sub>60</sub> incorporation on the crystallinity and interlayer spacing of MXene. As compared in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202400185-fig-0002">2a</a>, the intense diffraction peak at 39.1° of the MAX phase Ti<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> disappears in MXene, indicating that the Al layer has been selectively etched. The (002) diffraction peak of Ti<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> at 9.5° shifts to 6.9° of the pristine MXene, indicating interlayer spacing expansion to 12.8&nbsp;Å. After f-C<sub>60</sub> incorporation, such a (002) diffraction peak further shifts negatively to 6.2° corresponding to an interlayer spacing of 14.1&nbsp;Å, which is 0.9&nbsp;Å larger than that of the pristine MXene. This result is consistent with the HR-TEM results, confirming the successful intercalation of f-C<sub>60</sub> molecules into the interlayer of MXene nanosheets and consequently enlarging the interlayer spacing of MXene nanosheets. Besides, no discernible diffraction peak of f-C<sub>60</sub> itself is detected in the XRD pattern of C<sub>60</sub>@MXene because f-C<sub>60</sub> itself is short-range ordered. No characteristic signal of TiO<sub>2</sub> is observed in the XRD patterns of both pristine MXene and C<sub>60</sub>@MXene hybrid, ensuring that Ti has not been oxidized before and after the hydrothermal process.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202400185-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6cde2964-bfa6-46de-a85f-09b038ecd1dc/adfm202400185-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/6cde2964-bfa6-46de-a85f-09b038ecd1dc/adfm202400185-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ce8421dd-73e1-4b92-bb67-496fe82c3a58/adfm202400185-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6cde2964-bfa6-46de-a85f-09b038ecd1dc/adfm202400185-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202400185-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202400185" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD patterns of Ti<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub>, MXene, f-C<sub>60</sub> and C<sub>60</sub>@MXene along with the zoom patterns in the region of 5–8 degree. b) N 1s XPS spectra with the fitting results of C<sub>60</sub>@MXene. c) Ti L-edge XAS spectra of MXene and C<sub>60</sub>@MXene. The dashed rectangle and arrow mark the shift of the onset position of Ti L-edge of MXene and C<sub>60</sub>@MXene. d) Nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherms of MXene and C<sub>60</sub>@MXene.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The successful hybridization of f-C<sub>60</sub> and MXene nanosheets was further confirmed by Raman (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information) and Fourier transform infrared (FT-IR) spectroscopic studies (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). In the Raman spectrum of the pristine MXene, there are several characteristic vibration modes, including A<sub>1g</sub> at 200 and 720&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> corresponding respectively to the out-of-plane modes of Ti and C atoms, E<sub>g</sub> at 275, 390, and 587&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> assigned as in-plane modes of Ti, C and T<sub>x</sub> atoms, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0007_R_d145876601e1032" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> f-C<sub>60</sub> exhibits two Raman peaks at 1360 and 1583&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding respectively to the D and G bands of a typical nanocarbon.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0047_R_d145876601e1042" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Nature">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After C<sub>60</sub> incorporation, all A<sub>1g</sub> and E<sub>g</sub> peaks of the pristine MXene are clearly observed in the Raman spectrum of C<sub>60</sub>@MXene, whereas the G band signal at 1583&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> of f-C<sub>60</sub> shift negatively to 1570&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> and D band at 1360&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> becomes broadened and almost invisible due to the small amount of f-C<sub>60</sub> intercalated into MXene.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0048_R_d145876601e1067" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Carbon N. Y.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This confirms the existence of C<sub>60</sub> moiety, for which the obvious red-shift of the G band signal suggests strong intermolecular interactions between MXene and C<sub>60</sub> as a result of electron transfer from MXene to C<sub>60</sub> due to the strong electron-withdrawing ability of C<sub>60</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0038_R_d145876601e1081" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Nano Res.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the FT-IR spectrum of MXene, three characteristic vibrational peaks are detected, including -OH asymmetric stretch vibration at 3445&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, -OH bending vibration at 1631&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> and Ti─O stretch vibration at 556&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0049_R_d145876601e1093" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For f-C<sub>60</sub>, three characteristic vibrational peaks at 1732, 1392, and 1093&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, correspond to the ─C═O stretching vibration, ─C─OH symmetric deformation vibration, and ─C─O stretching vibration, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0042_R_d145876601e1102" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Mater. Chem. A">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The FT-IR spectrum of the C<sub>60</sub>@MXene hybrid shows a superposition of signals of MXene and f-C<sub>60</sub>.</p>
            
            <p>X-ray photoemission spectroscopic (XPS) measurements were employed to elucidate the bonding nature of C<sub>60</sub> with MXene. While C, O, Ti, and F signals are detected in the XPS survey spectra of both pristine MXene and C<sub>60</sub>@MXene, a new peak correlated with N elements appears at 401.8&nbsp;eV for C<sub>60</sub>@MXene hybrid, confirming the existence of f-C<sub>60</sub> moiety (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). In the high-resolution N 1s XPS spectrum of the C<sub>60</sub>@MXene hybrid illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400185-fig-0002">2b</a>, a new peak at 396.8&nbsp;eV emerges, which can be assigned as newly formed N─Ti bonds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0050_R_d145876601e1132" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Nano-Micro Lett.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This statement is confirmed by the appearance of new signals corresponding to N─Ti bonds in the high-resolution Ti 2p XPS spectrum (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10d</a>, Supporting Information<b>)</b>. The C 1s and O 1s region of the high-resolution XPS spectra of C<sub>60</sub>@MXene also exhibit more significant changes than MXene, confirming successful bonding between C<sub>60</sub> and MXene (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>We further implemented synchrotron X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) to unveil the effect of C<sub>60</sub> incorporation on the electron structure of MXene. As compared in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400185-fig-0002">2c</a>, the Ti XAS L-edge spectra of both MXene and C<sub>60</sub>@MXene show two pairs of peaks correlated to Ti L<sub>3</sub>- and L<sub>2</sub>-edges, which is correspond respectively to the excitation of Ti 2p<sub>3/2</sub> (L<sub>3</sub>-edge) and Ti 2p<sub>1/2</sub> (L<sub>2</sub>-edge) core levels to an unoccupied Ti 3d state.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0051_R_d145876601e1176" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Adv. Mater. Interfaces">51</a>-<a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0054_R_d145876601e1176" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Owing to the ligand field induced by oxygen atoms of surface chemical groups, the L<sub>3</sub>- and L<sub>2</sub>-edge signals are split into two sub-peaks attributed to electronic states with t<sub>2g</sub> and e<sub>g</sub> symmetries. The e<sub>g</sub> band is highly sensitive to the change in the local environment because the Ti e<sub>g</sub> orbitals point directly to the 2p orbitals of the surrounding O atoms.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0052_R_d145876601e1195" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The onset energy position of Ti L-edge of C<sub>60</sub>@MXene experiences a positive shift, suggesting a higher oxidization state of Ti as a result of electron transfer from MXene to C<sub>60</sub> as discussed above. Besides, the e<sub>g</sub> band looks broader and is positively shifted by 0.2&nbsp;eV, and the L<sub>2</sub> e<sub>g</sub> to t<sub>2g</sub> peak intensity ratio increases from 0.94 to 1.05, which can be attributed to the change of the oxygen coordination environment of surface Ti atoms caused by chemical bonding with f-C<sub>60</sub>. These results confirm electron transfer from MXene to C<sub>60</sub> through N─Ti bonds, and this may facilitate charge transportation in SIB as discussed below.</p>
            
            <p>The Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) specific surface areas of the pristine MXene and C<sub>60</sub>@MXene hybrid were determined by N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400185-fig-0002">2d</a>). C<sub>60</sub>@MXene exhibits a much larger BET-specific surface area (88.2 m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) than that of MXene (33.6 m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). C<sub>60</sub>@MXene has a broader pore size distribution in the range from 2 to 40&nbsp;nm than MXene, indicating more mesopores and micropores (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). The increased BET-specific surface area and pore size are result from the enlarged interlayer spacing of MXene and improved dispersion of nanosheets due to the intercalation of C<sub>60</sub> molecules into MXene interlayers.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202400185-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202400185-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Properties of C<sub>60</sub>@MXene as SIB Anode</h3>
            
            <p>Enlarged interlayer spacing of MXene nanosheets induced by C<sub>60</sub> intercalation stimulates us to apply the C<sub>60</sub>@MXene hybrid as the novel anode of SIBs. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202400185-fig-0003">3a</a> presents the galvanostatic discharge/charge curves of C<sub>60</sub>@MXene measured at a current density of 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, demonstrating the pseudocapacitive behavior in the Na-ion storage process (see Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12a</a>, Supporting Information, for the galvanostatic discharge/charge curves of the pristine MXene).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0055_R_d145876601e1275" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - ACS Nano">55</a>-<a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0063_R_d145876601e1275" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - J. Mater. Sci.">63</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> C<sub>60</sub>@MXene exhibits initial discharge/charge capacities of 338.7/149.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, which are comparable to those of the pristine MXene. The initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) of the C<sub>60</sub>@MXene hybrid reaches 44.0% at the first cycle during which solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) forms, which is higher than that of MXene (41.3%). In the following cycles, the capacities are rationally reversible, indicating that electrochemical reversibility can be established in the first few cycles.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202400185-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b6eaaadd-f6fc-49a4-a9ad-40e9b795c904/adfm202400185-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b6eaaadd-f6fc-49a4-a9ad-40e9b795c904/adfm202400185-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/07797840-2663-42d3-8ade-65c56935306a/adfm202400185-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b6eaaadd-f6fc-49a4-a9ad-40e9b795c904/adfm202400185-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202400185-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202400185" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Galvanostatic discharge/charge curves of C<sub>60</sub>@MXene electrode at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. b) the cycling performance of MXene and C<sub>60</sub>@MXene electrodes at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. c) Rate performance of MXene and C<sub>60</sub>@MXene at different current densities. d) long-cycling performance of C<sub>60</sub>@MXene electrode at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. e) Nyquist plots of MXene and C<sub>60</sub>@MXene.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The cycling performance of the C<sub>60</sub>@MXene hybrid anode was investigated at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and compared with that of the pristine MXene (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400185-fig-0003">3b</a>). It is impressive that the capacity of the C<sub>60</sub>@MXene anode increases gradually from 159.5 to 226.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 300 cycles. The pristine MXene shows similar behavior, while the eventual capacity after 300 cycles is only 188.3&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. The capacities of both anodes decrease sharply during the first 20 cycles, due likely to the irreversible capture of Na ions by the defects.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0021_R_d145876601e1341" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The enlarged interlayer spacing within the C<sub>60</sub>@MXene hybrid enables more abundant electrochemical active sites for Na<sup>+</sup> transportation, resulting in increased capacity of the C<sub>60</sub>@MXene hybrid.</p>
            
            <p>To further evaluate the electrochemical performances of C<sub>60</sub>@MXene, rate performance and long-cycling stability were measured. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400185-fig-0003">3c</a> compares the rate performances of the pristine MXene and C<sub>60</sub>@MXene anodes at different current densities from 0.1 to 5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. The results indicate that the C<sub>60</sub>@MXene electrode has superior high-rate capacities of 156.7, 146.3, 138.9, 128.4, 117.3 and 99.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0 and 5.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. When the current density returns to 0.1 from 5.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, a reversible capacity of 180.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> is obtained, with a capacity retention of 156.7% relative to that during the initial cycle at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Obviously, the capacities of C<sub>60</sub>@MXene hybrid at all rates are much higher than those of the pristine MXene. This result can be attributed to the fact that the C<sub>60</sub>@MXene is conducive to more rapid charge transport. Besides, the electrochemical properties of pure f-C<sub>60</sub> and 2wt%-C<sub>60</sub>@MXene (prepared from a raw material containing a blend of f-C<sub>60</sub> and Mxene with a mass ratio of 2:98) were also investigated for comparison with the pristine MXene and C<sub>60</sub>@MXene (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). Pure f-C<sub>60</sub> exhibits inferior electrochemical performance, indicating that the performance improvement of C<sub>60</sub>@MXene relative to the pristine MXene is due to f-C<sub>60</sub>-induced structural change of MXene instead of f-C<sub>60</sub> itself. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400185-fig-0003">3d</a> shows the excellent long-term cycling stability and the corresponding coulombic efficiency of the C<sub>60</sub>@MXene anode. The C<sub>60</sub>@MXene exhibits superior long-term cycling stability with a reversible capacity of ≈104&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (94.5% capacity retention) with a 100% coulombic efficiency after 10&nbsp;000 cycles, while the MXene electrode exhibits significant capacity decay after ≈2000 cycles. C<sub>60</sub> intercalated into MXene layers results in enlarged interlayer spacing, consequently retarding self-restacking during Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and deintercalation processes, thus improving the cycling performance. Compared to other MXene/carbon composites applied in SIB, C<sub>60</sub>@MXene exhibits excellent long-term cyclic stability after 10&nbsp;000 cycles and a decent capacity at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>Electrochemical impedance spectra (EIS) were measured to understand the excellent rate performance and cycling stability of C<sub>60</sub>@MXene. The Nyquist plots (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400185-fig-0003">3e</a>) show a semicircle at the middle-high frequency region, which is associated with the electrolyte resistance (R<sub>e</sub>) and charge transfer resistance (R<sub>ct</sub>) at the interface of electrode/electrolyte, and a straight line at the low-frequency region corresponds to Warburg impedance (W<sub>0</sub>) ascribed to the sodium diffusion through the electrode material, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0064_R_d145876601e1447" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 64 - Adv. Mater.">64</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400185-fig-0003">3e</a>, the R<sub>ct</sub> value of C<sub>60</sub>@MXene is 15.40&nbsp;Ω, which is much smaller than that of MXene (44.29&nbsp;Ω), indicating that the C<sub>60</sub>@MXene has facilitated Na<sup>+</sup> ions transport and electron transfer.</p>
            
            <p>The Na-ion storage performance was further investigated by using the coin-type half-cells in the potential window of 0.01–2.7&nbsp;V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). According to the first three cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of C<sub>60</sub>@MXene at 0.1&nbsp;mV<sub>&nbsp;</sub>s<sup>−1</sup>, the cathodic peaks at the range of 0.12─1.70&nbsp;V of the first CV curve show the formation of SEI film and/or the decomposition of electrolyte (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202400185-fig-0004">4a</a>). During the second and third cycles, the CV curves are almost overlapped, confirming the good cycling stability. The current responses in CV tests are recorded at scan rates from 0.1 to 1.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> to assess the electrochemical kinetics of MXene and C<sub>60</sub>@MXene anodes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400185-fig-0004">4b</a>, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12b</a>, Supporting Information). The shape of the CV curves for the two anodes is well preserved at the increasing scan rates, indicating good rate capability and small electrode polarization.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202400185-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/13a16b73-9165-4ad5-beac-505f3bb6d7d0/adfm202400185-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/13a16b73-9165-4ad5-beac-505f3bb6d7d0/adfm202400185-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/513aaafe-951c-4569-89a8-281cf3c9751c/adfm202400185-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/13a16b73-9165-4ad5-beac-505f3bb6d7d0/adfm202400185-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202400185-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202400185" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves of C<sub>60</sub>@MXene electrode under initial three cycles at sweep rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. b) CV curves of C<sub>60</sub>@MXene electrode at different scan rates. c) the plots of log (i) versus log (v) calculated from CV curves. Inset: (a) summary of the calculated (b) values. d) the contribution of capacitive and diffusion-controlled processes of C<sub>60</sub>@MXene at different scan rates.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To qualitatively evaluate the capacitive contribution, the kinetic behaviors of Na-ion storage were analyzed according to the mathematical equation of <i>i</i>  =  <i>av<sup>b</sup></i>. Here, <i>i</i> is the peak current, <i>v</i> is the scan rate, <i>a</i> is a constant, and the fitting slope (b-value) of the plots is well established as an indicator for the electrochemical kinetics that ranges from 0.5 (diffusion-controlled process) to 1 (surface-controlled or capacitive process).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0021_R_d145876601e1537" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The <b><i>b</i></b> values of the oxidation and reduction peaks in the C<sub>60</sub>@MXene are close to 1 (0.901 and 0.915) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400185-fig-0004">4c</a>), suggesting the ultra-fast Na<sup>+</sup> ion storage kinetics of C<sub>60</sub>@MXene is governed by a fast surface-controlled capacitive feature. Moreover, based on Equation <i>i</i>  = <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> <i>v</i> + <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>, the specific capacity can be quantificationally divided into the capacitive (<i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i>) effects and diffusion-controlled behavior (<i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>). The capacitive contribution of C<sub>60</sub>@MXene gradually increases from 63% to 85% as the scan rate increases from 0.1 to 1.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400185-fig-0004">4d</a>). The results indicate that the enlarged interlayer spacing and the facilitated charge transfer, due to the C<sub>60</sub> intercalation, sensitively affect the pseudocapacitive contribution of C<sub>60</sub>@MXene electrodes.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202400185-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202400185-sec-0050-title">2.3 Na<sup>+</sup> Storage Mechanism of C<sub>60</sub>@MXene Studied by First-Principle Calculations</h3>
            
            <p>Density functional theory (DFT) calculations were performed to further understand the storage mechanism of Na<sup>+</sup> ions in the confinement space between the layers of MXene and C<sub>60</sub>@MXene. For C<sub>60</sub>@MXene, a model with an intercalated C<sub>60</sub> existing between the MXene sheets was used. The structural model of the pristine MXene was fully optimized by setting F and O terminals according to our experimental conditions, resulting in an (002) plane spacing of 9.40&nbsp;Å. After the intercalation of C<sub>60</sub>, the spacing was increased to 18.15&nbsp;Å for C<sub>60</sub>@MXene. The adsorption energy of Na<sup>+</sup> in the confined interlayer space between MXene sheets is calculated to be −1.06&nbsp;eV, which becomes −1.91&nbsp;eV after C<sub>60</sub> intercalation as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202400185-fig-0005">5a,b</a> The nudged elastic band (NEB) method<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0065_R_d145876601e1626" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 65 - J. Chem. Phys.">65</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> was used to unveil the diffusion barrier of Na<sup>+</sup> in MXene and C<sub>60</sub>@MXene. In order to simplify the simulation, C<sub>60</sub> molecules are not explicitly considered during NEB calculations, instead, the enlarged interlayer spacing induced by C<sub>60</sub> intercalation is considered. The migration paths of sodium ions from one adsorption site to the nearest neighboring site in MXene and C<sub>60</sub>@MXene were calculated, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400185-fig-0005">5c</a>. The reaction energy path has shown a migration energy barrier of 0.45&nbsp;eV on the pristine MXene and 0.18&nbsp;eV on C<sub>60</sub>@MXene. After C<sub>60</sub> intercalation, the energy barrier significantly decreases to 0.27&nbsp;eV. The lower diffusion barriers of Na<sup>+</sup> between the C<sub>60</sub>@MXene interlayer imply faster diffusion processes. Since the fast charging/discharging ability is related to the diffusion behavior of Na in anode material, this result agrees well with the improved rate performance as discussed above.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202400185-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f89f9b66-bb07-4686-97a8-9f7faaba099e/adfm202400185-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/f89f9b66-bb07-4686-97a8-9f7faaba099e/adfm202400185-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/584c70b8-c5c5-4caa-bfee-f7b1bee547a3/adfm202400185-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f89f9b66-bb07-4686-97a8-9f7faaba099e/adfm202400185-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202400185-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202400185" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">DFT calculations for the Na-ion storage on the MXene and C<sub>60</sub>@ MXene. Optimized structural model with one absorbed Na<sup>+</sup> ion of a) MXene and b) C<sub>60</sub>@MXene. Na<sup>+</sup>-ion diffusion pathway between c) MXene interlayers and d) C<sub>60</sub>@MXene interlayers. e) diffusion profiles of the Na between MXene and C<sub>60</sub>@MXene interlayer in the NEB calculations.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202400185-sec-0060">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202400185-sec-0060-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we covalently intercalate fullerene C<sub>60</sub> molecules into the interlayer of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>x</sub> MXene nanosheets for the first time by using a low-temperature hydrothermal method, affording a novel C<sub>60</sub>@MXene hybrid. Successful intercalation of C<sub>60</sub> into MXene as pillars via N-Ti bonds is confirmed by a series of spectroscopic and morphological characterizations, which further reveal that intercalation of C<sub>60</sub> leads to the enlarged interlayer spacing of MXene nanosheets from 12.8 to 14.1&nbsp;Å, benefiting the diffusion of electrolyte and Na<sup>+</sup> ions. Using C<sub>60</sub>@MXene hybrid as a novel anode, the SIB delivers a specific capacity of 226.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 300 cycles, which surpasses that obtained from the pristine MXene anode, and retains 94.5% capacity at 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 10&nbsp;000 cycles. The improved specific capacity and cycling stability after C<sub>60</sub> intercalation are attributed to the facilitated Na<sup>+</sup> migrations resulting from C<sub>60</sub>-induced enlarged interlayer spacing, as confirmed by DFT simulations. Our present work demonstrates the effectiveness of fullerene intercalation in improving the electrochemical performance of MXene, paving the way for applications of 2D materials in electrochemical energy storage.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202400185-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202400185-sec-0070-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202400185-sec-0080">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202400185-sec-0090">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202400185-sec-0100">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202400185-sec-0100-title"> Materials</h4>
                  
                  <p>Ti<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> was purchased from 11 Technology Co., Ltd. Lithium fluoride (LiF, 99.99%) was purchased from Alfa Aesar. Hydrochloric Acid (HCl, 35–37%), hydrogen peroxide (H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>), ammonium hydroxide (NH<sub>3</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O), absolute ethanol (EtOH, 99.5%) and methanol (MeOH) were purchased from Sinopharm Chemical Reagent Co., Ltd. C<sub>60</sub> was purchased from Suzhou DCN Co., Ltd. All chemicals were used without further purification.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202400185-sec-0110">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202400185-sec-0110-title"> Synthesis of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>Tx MXene</h4>
                  
                  <p>First, 40&nbsp;mL of 9&nbsp;m&nbsp;HCl was added to a Teflon beaker. Then, 3.2&nbsp;g of LiF was added to the beaker under stirring for 30&nbsp;min. After that, 2&nbsp;g of Ti<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> was carefully and slowly added into the above mixture solution under an ice bath to avoid initial overheating of the solution. Then the mixture was constantly stirred at 35&nbsp;°C for 24&nbsp;h, and the obtained resultant was repeatedly washed with ultrapure water and centrifuged several times at 3500&nbsp;rpm to remove unreacted acid and impurities until the PH exceeded 6. Then the precipitate was added to 100&nbsp;mL of deionized water, followed by ultrasonic treatment for 6&nbsp;h. Finally, the supernatant was collected by centrifugation at 3500&nbsp;rpm for 1&nbsp;h and then collected as black powder by freeze-drying.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202400185-sec-0120">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202400185-sec-0120-title"> Synthesis of f-C<sub>60</sub></h4>
                  
                  <p>C<sub>60</sub> (300&nbsp;mg) was added to 25&nbsp;mL of 30&nbsp;wt % H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and 10&nbsp;mL of 28&nbsp;wt % NH<sub>3</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O. The suspension was vigorously stirred at 60&nbsp;°C under ambient conditions for 24&nbsp;h. The black-brown suspension gradually dissolved and turned into a yellow solution during the reaction. After centrifugation, the yellow supernatant was collected, and then an excessive amount of anhydrous ethanol was added to the solution to produce a yellowish product and remove the excess H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and NH<sub>3</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O. The precipitate waswashed several times with anhydrous ethanol and then dried in a vacuum oven at 40&nbsp;°C for 12&nbsp;h.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202400185-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202400185-sec-0130-title"> Synthesis of C<sub>60</sub>@MXene Hybrid</h4>
                  
                  <p>Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>x</sub> (120&nbsp;mg) MXene and 120&nbsp;mg f-C<sub>60</sub> were dispersed in 40&nbsp;mL ultrapure water followed by ultrasonic treatment for 2&nbsp;h and bubbled by Ar for 1&nbsp;h to remove dissolved O<sub>2</sub> from water. After that, the suspension was transferred into a 100&nbsp;mL Teflon-lined stainless-steel autoclave and heated at 95&nbsp;°C for 12&nbsp;h. After cooling to room temperature, the product was collected by centrifugation and washed with ultrapure water and methanol several times to remove unreacted f-C<sub>60</sub>. Then the black precipitate was collected by freeze-drying. 2&nbsp;wt%-C<sub>60</sub>@MXene was prepared through the same method with a 2:98 mass ratio of f-C<sub>60</sub> and MXene.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202400185-sec-0140">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202400185-sec-0140-title"> Electrochemical Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <p>The Electrochemical properties of electrodes were investigated based on coin-type cells (CR2032). The active material, carbon black and poly(vinylidene fluoride) (PVDF) were dispersed in N-methyl-2-pyrolidinone (NMP) at a ratio of 8:1:1, which was coated on the Al foil (conductive carbon coated) and dried under vacuum at 60&nbsp;°C for 12&nbsp;h. The loading and diameter of the working electrode were 0.5–0.8&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>─2</sup> and 10&nbsp;mm, respectively. The electrolyte was 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span>&nbsp;NaPF<sub>6</sub> in diethylene glycol dimethyl ether (DEGDME) sodium foil was used as the counter and reference electrode and a Celgard 2325 separator was used as the separator. The coin cells were assembled in a glove box filled with argon. The galvanostatic charge/discharge measurements were performed on the Neware battery testing system (CT-4008T, Shenzhen, China) in the voltage range from 0.01 to 2.7&nbsp;V at different current densities. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) were performed on a PARSTAT MC electrochemical workstation (Princeton Company, USA). CV was measured with a series of scanning rates from 0.1 to 10&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> within a potential range from 0.01 to 2.7&nbsp;V, and the EIS measurements were conducted in a frequency range of 100&nbsp;kHz-10&nbsp;mHz.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202400185-sec-0150">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202400185-sec-0150-title"> Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images were obtained with a field-emission scanning electron microscope (FEI Apero). Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images were collected from Hitachi H-7650 transmission electron microscope using an accelerating voltage of 100&nbsp;kV. The high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) and high-angle annular dark-field scanning transmission electron microscopy (HAADF-STEM) and corresponding energy-dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) mapping analyses were conducted on a JEM F200 microscope operating at a voltage of 200&nbsp;kV. AFM measurements were performed on an XE7 scanning probe microscope (Park, Korea). The X-ray powder diffraction (XRD) patterns were obtained using a Rigaku Smart Lab X-ray diffraction instrument (Rigaku, Japan) with Cu-Ka radiation (0.154&nbsp;nm). Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectra were performed on a TENSOR 27 spectrometer (Bruker, Germany) at room temperature. The Raman spectra were carried out in Via Raman Microscope equipment (Renishaw, England) with a 532&nbsp;nm excitation laser. The specific surface area was evaluated at 77&nbsp;K (Micromeritics ASAP 2020) using the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) method applied to the adsorption branch. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was conducted on a Thermo-VV ESCALAB 250 (Thermo-VV Scientific) instrument with a monochromatized Al Ka X-ray source in a vacuum. The angular-dependent C K-edge X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) was measured on beamline BL12B of the National Synchrotron Radiation Laboratory, Hefei, using the total electron yield mode by collecting the sample drain current under a chamber pressure of &lt;10<sup>−7</sup>&nbsp;Pa. The energy range was 100−1000&nbsp;eV with an energy resolution of ca. 0.2&nbsp;eV.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202400185-sec-0160">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202400185-sec-0160-title"> Calculating Details</h4>
                  
                  <div class="paragraph-element">The DFT<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0066" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0066_R_d145876601e1873" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 66 - John Wiley Sons">66</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> calculations were carried out using the projector-augmented wave pseudopotentials<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0067" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0067_R_d145876601e1878" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 67 - Phys. Rev. B">67</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> as adopted in the Vienna ab initio simulation package (VASP).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0068" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0068_R_d145876601e1883" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 68 - Phys. Rev. B">68</a>-<a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0070" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0070_R_d145876601e1883" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 70 - Phys. Rev. B">70</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Exchange correlation interactions were described by the Perdew–Burke–Ernzerhof (PBE)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0071" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0071_R_d145876601e1889" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 71 - Phys. Rev. Lett.">71</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> generalized gradient approximation (GGA).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400185-bib-0072" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400185-bib-0072_R_d145876601e1894" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 72 - Phys. Rev. B">72</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The plane wave kinetic energy cutoff was set to 400&nbsp;eV. Brillouin zone was represented by Gamma special k-point mesh of 3&nbsp;×&nbsp;3&nbsp;×&nbsp;1 for calculations. To ensure stable geometries, the force convergence was set to be 0.01&nbsp;eV&nbsp;Å<sup>−1,</sup> and the structural optimization convergence criteria was set to be 10<sup>−5</sup>&nbsp;eV in energy. The adsorption energies of Na-adsorbed MXene and C<sub>60</sub>@MXene were defined by the following equation:
